Chapter 35: Research Methodology

Section A: Basic Research Concepts

Types of Research

1. Observational Studies - Cross-sectional: Single point in time, examines prevalence - Case-control: Retrospective comparison of exposed/unexposed groups - Cohort: Prospective follow-up of exposed vs unexposed groups

2. Experimental Studies - Randomized Controlled Trials (RCT): Gold standard for interventions - Quasi-experimental: No randomization but intervention given - Community trials: Population-level interventions

Research Question Formulation

FINER Criteria: - Feasible - Interesting - Novel - Ethical - Relevant

PICO Framework: - Population/Problem - Intervention - Comparison - Outcome

Hypothesis Testing

Null Hypothesis (H‚ÇÄ): - States no association/effect - Default assumption in research

Alternative Hypothesis (H‚ÇÅ): - States presence of association/effect - What researcher aims to prove

Variable Definitions

Independent Variable: - Cause or predictor variable - Researcher manipulates/selects

Dependent Variable: - Effect or outcome variable - Measured response

Confounding Variable: - Associated with both exposure and outcome - Can distort true relationship

Sampling Methods

Probability Sampling: - Simple Random: Equal chance for each unit - Systematic: Every nth unit selected - Stratified: Division into subgroups, random sampling from each - Cluster: Groups selected randomly, all units within included

Non-probability Sampling: - Convenience: Easily accessible subjects - Purposive: Based on researcher‚Äôs judgment - Snowball: Existing subjects recruit others

Sample Size Calculation

For Comparing Two Means: n = 2(ZŒ±/2 + ZŒ≤)¬≤œÉ¬≤ / Œ¥¬≤

Where: - ZŒ±/2 = 1.96 for 95% CI - ZŒ≤ = 0.84 for 80% power - œÉ = Standard deviation - Œ¥ = Minimum difference to detect

For Comparing Two Proportions: n = [ZŒ±/2‚àö(2pÃÑ(1-pÃÑ)) + ZŒ≤‚àö(p‚ÇÅ(1-p‚ÇÅ) + p‚ÇÇ(1-p‚ÇÇ))]¬≤ / (p‚ÇÅ-p‚ÇÇ)¬≤

Research Ethics and Consent

Basic Principles: - Autonomy: Respect for persons - Beneficence: Do good - Non-maleficence: Do no harm - Justice: Fair distribution

Informed Consent Elements: - Purpose and procedures - Risks and benefits - Alternative treatments - Confidentiality - Voluntary participation - Right to withdraw

Section B: Data Collection Methods

Questionnaire Design

Types of Questions: - Open-ended: Allows free responses - Closed-ended: Predefined response options - Dichotomous: Yes/No questions - Multiple choice: Select from options - Likert scale: Agreement ratings

Design Principles: - Clear and concise language - Avoid double-barreled questions - Logical sequence - Skip patterns for routing - Pilot testing essential

Interview Techniques

Structured Interview: - Predetermined questions - Standardized format - High reliability

Semi-structured Interview: - Key questions predetermined - Flexibility in follow-up - Balance of consistency and depth

Unstructured Interview: - Free-flowing conversation - Exploratory purpose - High depth but lower reliability

Observation Methods

Participant Observation: - Researcher actively participates - Immersive experience - High validity but potential bias

Non-participant Observation: - External observer role - Less bias - May miss contextual details

Observational Tools: - Checklists - Rating scales - Time and motion studies

Focus Group Discussions

Group Size: 6-10 participants Duration: 60-90 minutes Moderator Role: - Guide discussion - Manage group dynamics - Ensure all participate

Benefits: - Group interaction - Diverse perspectives - Cost-effective - Rich qualitative data

Secondary Data Sources

Types: - Census data: Population characteristics - Vital statistics: Birth and death records - Hospital records: Patient data - Survey data: National health surveys - Administrative records: Health service data

Advantages: - Cost-effective - Large sample sizes - Historical trends available

Limitations: - May not meet specific research needs - Data quality variable - Access restrictions

Data Quality Assurance

Validity: - Internal: Causal relationship validity - External: Generalizability

Reliability: - Consistency: Reproducibility - Stability: Test-retest reliability

Quality Control Measures: - Training of data collectors - Regular supervision - Double data entry - Range and logic checks

Section C: Data Analysis and Interpretation

Statistical Software Applications

Common Software: - SPSS: User-friendly, widely used in social sciences - R: Open-source, extensive statistical capabilities - STATA: Biostatistics focus - SAS: Enterprise-level data management - Epi Info: CDC-developed, epidemiology-specific

Descriptive Statistics

Measures of Central Tendency:

Mean: - Sum of all values divided by number of values - Most commonly used - Sensitive to outliers - Formula: Œº = Œ£x/n

Median: - Middle value when data arranged - Not sensitive to outliers - Better for skewed distributions

Mode: - Most frequently occurring value - Can have multiple modes

Measures of Dispersion:

Range: - Difference between maximum and minimum - Simple but limited

Variance (œÉ¬≤): - Average squared deviation from mean - Formula: œÉ¬≤ = Œ£(x-Œº)¬≤/n

Standard Deviation (œÉ): - Square root of variance - Same units as original data - Most commonly used measure

Inferential Statistics

t-test: - Compares means of two groups - Independent t-test: Different groups - Paired t-test: Same group before/after - One-sample t-test: Compare to known value

Chi-square (œá¬≤) Test: - Tests association between categorical variables - Formula: œá¬≤ = Œ£(O-E)¬≤/E - Degrees of freedom: (rows-1) √ó (columns-1)

ANOVA (Analysis of Variance): - Compares means of three or more groups - One-way ANOVA: One independent variable - Two-way ANOVA: Two independent variables

Regression Analysis

Linear Regression: - Models linear relationship between variables - Equation: Y = a + bX - Where Y = dependent, X = independent variable

Logistic Regression: - For binary outcome variables - Odds ratio interpretation - Common in epidemiology

Interpretation of Results

P-value Interpretation: - P < 0.05: Statistically significant - P < 0.01: Highly significant - P < 0.001: Very highly significant

Confidence Intervals: - Range likely to contain true value - 95% CI most commonly used - Indicates precision of estimate

Effect Size: - Practical significance beyond statistical significance - Cohen‚Äôs d, odds ratios, etc.

Report Writing and Presentation

Research Report Structure: 1. Title page 2. Abstract 3. Introduction 4. Methods 5. Results 6. Discussion 7. Conclusions 8. References 9. Appendices

Key Elements: - Clear objectives - Transparent methodology - Complete results - Balanced interpretation - Practical implications

Chapter 36: Biostatistics

Section A: Descriptive Statistics

Measures of Central Tendency

Mean (Average):

Properties: - Most sensitive measure - Affected by extreme values - Used for normally distributed data

Formula: Œº = Œ£x/n

Example: If systolic BP readings are: 120, 130, 140, 150, 160 Mean = (120+130+140+150+160)/5 = 140 mmHg

Median:

Properties: - Middle value when ordered - Less affected by outliers - Best for skewed distributions

Example: For data: 2, 4, 6, 8, 100 Median = 6 (not affected by outlier 100)

Mode:

Properties: - Most frequent value - May have multiple modes - Used for categorical data

Measures of Dispersion

Range:

Formula: Range = Maximum - Minimum

Example: Data: 10, 15, 20, 25, 30 Range = 30 - 10 = 20

Variance (œÉ¬≤):

Formula: œÉ¬≤ = Œ£(x-Œº)¬≤/n

Properties: - Average squared deviation - In squared units - Hard to interpret

Standard Deviation (œÉ):

Formula: œÉ = ‚àöœÉ¬≤

Properties: - Square root of variance - Same units as original data - Most commonly used

Interpretation: - About 68% data within 1 SD of mean - About 95% data within 2 SD of mean - About 99.7% data within 3 SD of mean

Percentiles and Quartiles

Percentiles: - Value below which certain percentage falls - 50th percentile = median - P90 = 90% values below this point

Quartiles: - Q1 = 25th percentile (first quartile) - Q2 = 50th percentile (median) - Q3 = 75th percentile (third quartile)

Interquartile Range (IQR): - IQR = Q3 - Q1 - Measures spread of middle 50% data - Less affected by outliers

Data Visualization

Types of Graphs:

Bar Chart: - Categorical data - Discrete categories

Histogram: - Continuous data - Shows frequency distribution

Line Graph: - Trends over time - Continuous data

Scatter Plot: - Relationship between two variables - Shows correlation

Box Plot: - Shows median, quartiles, outliers - Good for comparing distributions

Normal Distribution Properties

Characteristics: - Bell-shaped curve - Symmetrical around mean - Mean = Median = Mode

68-95-99.7 Rule: - 68% within 1 SD of mean - 95% within 2 SD of mean - 99.7% within 3 SD of mean

Standard Normal Distribution: - Mean = 0 - Standard deviation = 1 - Z-scores convert any normal distribution

Z-score Formula: Z = (X - Œº) / œÉ

Where: - X = individual value - Œº = population mean - œÉ = population standard deviation

Section B: Inferential Statistics

Probability Concepts

Basic Probability: - P(A) = Number of favorable outcomes / Total outcomes - Range: 0 to 1 (or 0% to 100%) - P(A) + P(A‚Äô) = 1 (A‚Äô = not A)

Addition Rule: P(A ‚à™ B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A ‚à© B)

Multiplication Rule: P(A ‚à© B) = P(A) √ó P(B|A)

Hypothesis Testing

Steps in Hypothesis Testing:

  1. State hypotheses:

    • H‚ÇÄ (null): No effect/association
    • H‚ÇÅ (alternative): Effect/association exists

  2. Set significance level (Œ±):

    • Usually 0.05 (5%)

  3. Choose test statistic:

    • t-test, chi-square, z-test, etc.

  4. Calculate test statistic

  5. Find p-value

  6. Make decision:

    • If p ‚â§ Œ±, reject H‚ÇÄ
    • If p > Œ±, fail to reject H‚ÇÄ

Type I Error (Œ±): - Reject H‚ÇÄ when it‚Äôs true - False positive - Probability = significance level

Type II Error (Œ≤): - Fail to reject H‚ÇÄ when it‚Äôs false - False negative - Power = 1 - Œ≤

Confidence Intervals

Formula for Mean (Large Sample): CI = xÃÑ ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó (s/‚àön)

Where: - xÃÑ = sample mean - Z(Œ±/2) = 1.96 for 95% CI - s = sample standard deviation - n = sample size

Formula for Proportion: CI = p ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó ‚àö[p(1-p)/n]

Interpretation: 95% CI means 95% of such intervals will contain true population parameter

P-values and Statistical Significance

P-value: - Probability of obtaining results as extreme as observed, assuming H‚ÇÄ is true - Smaller p-value = stronger evidence against H‚ÇÄ

Significance Levels: - p < 0.05: Significant () - p < 0.01: Highly significant () - p < 0.001: Very highly significant ()

Statistical Tests Selection

Parametric Tests: - Assume normal distribution - More powerful - Require interval/ratio data - Examples: t-test, ANOVA, Pearson correlation

Non-parametric Tests: - No distribution assumptions - Less powerful - Use ordinal data - Examples: Mann-Whitney U, Kruskal-Wallis, Spearman correlation

Test Selection Guide:

Data Type Groups Parametric Non-parametric
Continuous 2 t-test Mann-Whitney U
Continuous 3+ ANOVA Kruskal-Wallis
Ordinal 2 t-test Mann-Whitney U
Ordinal 3+ ANOVA Kruskal-Wallis
Categorical 2 Chi-square Fisher‚Äôs exact
Continuous Correlation Pearson Spearman

Section C: Advanced Statistics

Correlation and Regression

Pearson Correlation (r): - Measures linear relationship between two continuous variables - Range: -1 to +1 - r = 1: Perfect positive correlation - r = 0: No linear correlation - r = -1: Perfect negative correlation

Formula: r = Œ£[(x-xÃÑ)(y-»≥)] / ‚àö[Œ£(x-xÃÑ)¬≤ √ó Œ£(y-»≥)¬≤]

Spearman Rank Correlation: - Non-parametric alternative - Uses ranks instead of actual values - Good for ordinal data or non-linear relationships

Linear Regression:

Simple Linear Regression: Y = a + bX

Where: - a = y-intercept - b = slope = r √ó (s_y/s_x)

Multiple Linear Regression: Y = a + b‚ÇÅX‚ÇÅ + b‚ÇÇX‚ÇÇ + ‚Ä¶ + b‚ÇôX‚Çô

ANOVA (Analysis of Variance)

One-way ANOVA: - Compares means of 3+ independent groups - Assumptions: normality, homogeneity of variance, independence

Two-way ANOVA: - Two independent variables - Tests main effects and interaction

ANOVA Table:

Source SS df MS F p-value
Between SSB k-1 MSB = SSB/(k-1) MSB/MSW
Within SSW N-k MSW = SSW/(N-k)
Total SST N-1

Survival Analysis

Key Concepts: - Survival time: Time until event occurs - Censoring: Incomplete observation - Hazard function: Instantaneous risk rate

Kaplan-Meier Method: - Non-parametric method for survival curves - Handles censoring naturally - Log-rank test compares survival curves

Cox Proportional Hazards Model: - Regression model for survival data - Hazard ratio interpretation - Assumes proportional hazards

Logistic Regression

For Binary Outcomes: - Disease vs no disease - Exposed vs unexposed

Logit Model: logit(p) = ln[p/(1-p)] = b‚ÇÄ + b‚ÇÅX‚ÇÅ + b‚ÇÇX‚ÇÇ + ‚Ä¶

Odds Ratio Interpretation: - OR = 1: No association - OR > 1: Increased odds - OR < 1: Decreased odds

Example: If OR for smoking and lung cancer = 10 Interpretation: Smokers have 10 times higher odds of lung cancer

Meta-analysis Basics

Purpose: - Combine results from multiple studies - Get overall effect estimate - Resolve conflicting results

Steps: 1. Define research question 2. Search for studies 3. Select relevant studies 4. Extract data 5. Analyze and combine results

Forest Plot: - Visual representation of meta-analysis results - Shows individual study results - Shows pooled estimate with confidence interval

Sample Size Calculations

For Comparing Two Means:

n = 2(ZŒ±/2 + ZŒ≤)¬≤œÉ¬≤ / Œ¥¬≤

Where: - ZŒ±/2 = 1.96 for 95% confidence - ZŒ≤ = 0.84 for 80% power - œÉ = standard deviation - Œ¥ = minimum difference to detect

For Comparing Two Proportions:

n = [ZŒ±/2‚àö(2pÃÑ(1-pÃÑ)) + ZŒ≤‚àö(p‚ÇÅ(1-p‚ÇÅ) + p‚ÇÇ(1-p‚ÇÇ))]¬≤ / (p‚ÇÅ-p‚ÇÇ)¬≤

For Correlation Studies:

n = [(ZŒ±/2 + ZŒ≤)/0.5 √ó ln((1+r)/(1-r))]¬≤ + 3

Chapter 37: Social Work and Demography

Section A: Demography

Population Dynamics

Key Concepts: - Population: Group of individuals in a defined area - Demography: Study of population size, composition, and distribution - Population dynamics: Changes in population over time

Population Change Formula: P‚ÇÅ = P‚ÇÄ + (B - D) + (I - E)

Where: - P‚ÇÅ = Population at time 1 - P‚ÇÄ = Population at time 0 - B = Births - D = Deaths - I = Immigration - E = Emigration

Demographic Transition Model

Stage 1: Pre-transition (High Stationary) - High birth rate - High death rate - Low growth rate - Pre-industrial societies

Stage 2: Early transition (Early Expanding) - Declining death rate - High birth rate - Rapid population growth - Improving healthcare, sanitation

Stage 3: Late transition (Late Expanding) - Declining birth rate - Low death rate - Slowing growth - Industrial societies

Stage 4: Post-transition (Low Stationary) - Low birth rate - Low death rate - Very low growth rate - Post-industrial societies

Stage 5: Declining - Birth rate below death rate - Population decline - Some developed countries

Population Pyramids

Types of Population Pyramids:

Expansive Pyramid: - Wide base (high birth rates) - Narrow top (low life expectancy) - Rapid population growth - Developing countries pattern

Stationary Pyramid: - Straight sides - Moderate birth and death rates - Stable population - Developed countries pattern

Constrictive Pyramid: - Narrow base (low birth rates) - Larger middle/upper sections - Aging population - Post-industrial societies

Vital Statistics

Crude Birth Rate (CBR): CBR = (Number of births in a year / Mid-year population) √ó 1000

Crude Death Rate (CDR): CDR = (Number of deaths in a year / Mid-year population) √ó 1000

Growth Rate: Growth Rate = CBR - CDR + (Immigration - Emigration)

Total Fertility Rate (TFR): Average number of children a woman would have during her lifetime

Infant Mortality Rate (IMR): IMR = (Deaths under 1 year / Live births) √ó 1000

Maternal Mortality Ratio (MMR): MMR = (Maternal deaths / Live births) √ó 100,000

Census Data Interpretation

Census Questions: - Age and sex distribution - Marital status - Education levels - Occupation and employment - Migration patterns - Housing conditions

Key Indicators: - Sex ratio = Males/Females √ó 1000 - Dependency ratio = (0-14 + 65+) / (15-64) √ó 100 - Literacy rate = Literate population / Total population √ó 100

Population Projections

Methods:

Arithmetic Growth Method: P‚Çô = P‚ÇÄ(1 + nr)

Where: - P‚Çô = Population after n years - P‚ÇÄ = Initial population - r = growth rate - n = number of years

Geometric Growth Method: P‚Çô = P‚ÇÄ(1 + r)‚Åø

Cohort Component Method: - Most accurate method - Follows specific age cohorts - Considers births, deaths, migration

Section B: Social Work Principles

Social Work in Healthcare

Medical Social Work: - Liaison between healthcare system and patients - Address social determinants of health - Psychosocial assessment and intervention - Discharge planning - Community resource linkage

Roles of Medical Social Workers: - Patient and family counseling - Crisis intervention - Financial counseling - Rehabilitation support - End-of-life care planning

Healthcare Social Work Services: - Case management - Group therapy - Community outreach - Health education - Advocacy

Community Organization Methods

Community Organization Methods

Problem-solving Approach: - Problem identification and analysis - Resource assessment - Action planning and implementation - Evaluation and follow-up

Community Development: - Local leadership development - Capacity building - Community participation - Sustainable development

Social Planning: - Technical approach to social problems - Evidence-based interventions - Systematic planning process - Coordination of services

Social Case Work Principles

Core Principles: - Individualization - Purposeful expression of feeling - Controlled emotional involvement - Non-judgmental attitude - Confidentiality - Self-determination

Case Work Process: 1. Study: Assessment of situation 2. Diagnosis: Understanding the problem 3. Treatment: Intervention and support 4. Follow-up: Monitoring and evaluation

Interviewing Techniques: - Rapport building - Active listening - empathetic understanding - Goal setting - Resource mobilization

Group Work Approaches

Types of Groups:

Treatment Groups: - Focus on member problems - Therapeutic objectives - Professional leadership - Structured activities

Task Groups: - Goal-oriented - Problem-solving focus - Time-limited - Outcome-focused

Socialization Groups: - Skill development - Behavioral change - Peer support - Community integration

Group Work Principles: - Democratic participation - Mutual help and support - Group interaction - Individual growth within group context

Social Legislation and Welfare

Key Social Laws in India:

The Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act (2005): - Protection orders - Residence orders - Monetary relief - Custody orders

The Right to Education Act (2009): - Free and compulsory education - 25% reservation for disadvantaged children - Infrastructure requirements - Teacher-student ratios

The Mahatma Gandhi National Rural Employment Guarantee Act (2005): - 100 days employment guarantee - Rural development focus - Women participation priority - Social security measures

National Social Assistance Programme: - Old age pension - Widow pension - Disability pension - Family benefit schemes

Social Security Systems in India

Constitutional Provisions: - Article 41: Right to work, education, and public assistance - Article 42: Just and humane working conditions - Article 43: Living wage and decent standard of life

Social Security Categories:

Contributory Schemes: - Employees‚Äô State Insurance (ESI) - Provident Fund - Gratuity - Pension schemes

Non-contributory Schemes: - National Old Age Pension Scheme - Indira Gandhi National Widow Pension Scheme - Indira Gandhi National Disability Pension Scheme - National Family Benefit Scheme

Healthcare Security: - Employees‚Äô State Insurance Scheme - Central Government Health Scheme - Pradhan Mantri Jan Arogya Yojana (PMJAY) - State-specific health insurance schemes

Section C: Health Demography

Demographic and Health Surveys

National Family Health Survey (NFHS): - Demographic and health indicators - Reproductive and child health - Nutrition status - Immunization coverage - Healthcare utilization patterns

Sample Registration System (SRS): - Continuous data collection - Birth and death rates - Fertility and mortality estimates - Rural-urban differentials

District Level Household and Facility Survey (DLHS): - Healthcare infrastructure - Service utilization - Maternal and child health - Family planning services

Fertility and Mortality Patterns

Fertility Indicators:

Total Fertility Rate (TFR): - Replacement level: 2.1 children per woman - India‚Äôs current TFR: Approximately 2.0 - State variations: Kerala (1.8), Uttar Pradesh (2.4)

Age-specific Fertility Rate (ASFR): - Peak fertility in 20-24 age group - Declining trend in all age groups - Educational impact on fertility patterns

Mortality Patterns:

Infant Mortality Rate (IMR): - Current IMR: ~28 per 1000 live births - Rural vs urban differentials - State-level variations

Maternal Mortality Ratio (MMR): - Current MMR: ~97 per 100,000 live births - Leading causes: Hemorrhage, sepsis, hypertension - Prevention strategies importance

Migration and Health

Types of Migration: - Rural to Urban: Health service access challenges - Inter-state: Cultural and linguistic barriers - International: Health system adaptation - Internal displacement: Crisis-related health issues

Migration and Health Relationships: - Public health implications: Disease transmission patterns - Healthcare access: Continuity of care issues - Social determinants: Housing, education, employment - Vulnerable populations: Women, children, elderly

Health Challenges for Migrants: - Language barriers - Cultural differences - Economic constraints - Legal status issues - Social isolation

Urban vs Rural Health Patterns

Urban Health Characteristics: - Higher burden of NCDs - Better healthcare access - Environmental health issues - Lifestyle-related diseases - Mental health concerns

Rural Health Characteristics: - Higher maternal and child mortality - Infectious diseases prevalence - Limited healthcare infrastructure - Transportation challenges - Health literacy issues

Urban Health Challenges: - Air pollution - Water quality - Waste management - Housing density - Stress-related disorders

Rural Health Challenges: - Access barriers - Human resource shortages - Infrastructure limitations - Financial constraints - Traditional health practices

Health Transition

Epidemiological Transition: - Shift from infectious to chronic diseases - Changing disease patterns - Demographic transition impact - Lifestyle modification needs

Demographic Transition: - Aging population - Changing dependency ratios - Healthcare needs evolution - Social support systems

Nutritional Transition: - Nutritional deficiency to excess - Obesity emergence - Diet pattern changes - Physical activity patterns

Components of Health Transition: 1. Declining mortality rates 2. Changing disease patterns 3. Extended life expectancy 4. Modified health needs 5. Healthcare system adaptation

Policy Implications

National Health Policy (2017) Goals: - Health for all approach - Universal health coverage - Digital health initiatives - Traditional medicine integration

Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs): - SDG 3: Good health and well-being - SDG 5: Gender equality - SDG 10: Reduced inequalities - Interconnected goals impact

Evidence-Based Policy Making: - Research utilization - Data-driven decisions - Cost-effectiveness analysis - Implementation science

Examination Tips and Common Questions

Research Methodology - Quick Reference

Memory Aids: - FINER: Feasible, Interesting, Novel, Ethical, Relevant - PICO: Population, Intervention, Comparison, Outcome - OBSERVATIONAL: Cross-sectional, Case-control, Cohort, Ecological

Common Examination Questions:

  1. Sample Size Calculation:
    • Formula for two means: n = 2(ZŒ±/2 + ZŒ≤)¬≤œÉ¬≤ / Œ¥¬≤
    • For proportions: Use pooled proportion method
  2. Study Design Selection:
    • Intervention studies ‚Üí RCT
    • Prevalence ‚Üí Cross-sectional
    • Rare diseases ‚Üí Case-control
    • Rare exposures ‚Üí Cohort
  3. Bias Prevention:
    • Selection bias ‚Üí Randomization, blinding
    • Information bias ‚Üí Standardization, validation
    • Confounding ‚Üí Stratification, adjustment

Biostatistics - Quick Reference

Memory Aids: - Mean: Sensitive to outliers - Median: Robust to outliers - Mode: Most frequent - 68-95-99.7: Rule for normal distribution

Common Calculations:

Z-score: Z = (X - Œº) / œÉ

Confidence Interval for Mean: CI = xÃÑ ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó (s/‚àön)

Chi-square Test: œá¬≤ = Œ£(O-E)¬≤/E df = (rows-1) √ó (columns-1)

Test Selection Guide: - 2 groups, continuous data ‚Üí t-test - 3+ groups, continuous data ‚Üí ANOVA - Categorical data ‚Üí Chi-square - Correlation ‚Üí Pearson (parametric), Spearman (non-parametric)

Demography and Social Work - Quick Reference

Key Formulas: - CBR: (Births/Population) √ó 1000 - CDR: (Deaths/Population) √ó 1000 - Growth Rate: CBR - CDR + Net migration - Sex Ratio: (Males/Females) √ó 1000 - Dependency Ratio: ((0-14 + 65+)/(15-64)) √ó 100

Demographic Transition Stages: 1. High stationary (high birth + high death) 2. Early expanding (high birth + declining death) 3. Late expanding (declining birth + low death) 4. Low stationary (low birth + low death) 5. Declining (birth below death)

Social Work Principles: - Case Work: Individual focus - Group Work: Collective action - Community Work: Population level - CRM: Client, Resources, Methods

Practical Examples and Calculations

Research Methodology Examples

Sample Size Calculation Example: Problem: Calculate sample size to detect 5 mmHg difference in systolic BP between two groups, assuming SD = 15 mmHg, Œ± = 0.05, power = 80%

Solution: n = 2(1.96 + 0.84)¬≤ √ó 15¬≤ / 5¬≤ n = 2(7.84) √ó 225 / 25 n = 2 √ó 7.84 √ó 9 n = 141.12 ‚âà 142 per group

Biostatistics Examples

Descriptive Statistics Example: Data: Age of 10 patients: 25, 30, 35, 40, 45, 50, 55, 60, 65, 70

Mean = (25+30+35+40+45+50+55+60+65+70)/10 = 475/10 = 47.5 years

Median = (45+50)/2 = 47.5 years

Range = 70-25 = 45 years

Confidence Interval Example: Data: Sample mean = 120 mmHg, SD = 15 mmHg, n = 100

95% CI = 120 ¬± 1.96 √ó (15/‚àö100) 95% CI = 120 ¬± 1.96 √ó 1.5 95% CI = 120 ¬± 2.94 95% CI = (117.06, 122.94) mmHg

Chi-square Test Example: Problem: Association between smoking and lung cancer

Cancer No Cancer Total
Smokers 80 120 200
Non-smokers 20 180 200
Total 100 300 400

Expected frequencies: - Smokers with cancer: (200√ó100)/400 = 50 - Smokers without cancer: (200√ó300)/400 = 150 - Non-smokers with cancer: (200√ó100)/400 = 50 - Non-smokers without cancer: (200√ó300)/400 = 150

Chi-square calculation: œá¬≤ = (80-50)¬≤/50 + (120-150)¬≤/150 + (20-50)¬≤/50 + (180-150)¬≤/150 œá¬≤ = 900/50 + 900/150 + 900/50 + 900/150 œá¬≤ = 18 + 6 + 18 + 6 = 48

df = (2-1) √ó (2-1) = 1 p < 0.001 (highly significant)

Demography Examples

Population Growth Calculation: Problem: Population in 2020 = 1 billion, CBR = 20/1000, CDR = 8/1000, net migration = 100,000

Annual increase = (20-8)/1000 √ó 1,000,000,000 + 100,000 = 12/1000 √ó 1,000,000,000 + 100,000 = 12,000,000 + 100,000 = 12,100,000

Growth rate = 12,100,000 / 1,000,000,000 = 0.0121 = 1.21%

Sex Ratio Calculation: Problem: Males = 51,000, Females = 49,000

Sex ratio = (51,000/49,000) √ó 1000 = 1041 males per 1000 females

Dependency Ratio Calculation: Problem: Age 0-14 = 25,000, Age 15-64 = 60,000, Age 65+ = 5,000

Dependency ratio = ((25,000 + 5,000)/60,000) √ó 100 = (30,000/60,000) √ó 100 = 50%

Memory Aids for Formulas and Concepts

Statistical Formulas - Mnemonics

Mean Formula: ‚ÄúSum of X divided by N‚Äù (Œ£x/n) - Remember: ‚ÄúS*N‚Äù = ‚ÄúSum divided by Number‚Äù

Standard Deviation: ‚ÄúSquare root of variance‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúœÉ = ‚àöœÉ¬≤‚Äù = ‚ÄúSigma equals square root of sigma squared‚Äù

Z-score: ‚ÄúX minus mu over sigma‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúZ = (X-Œº)/œÉ‚Äù = ‚ÄúZ equals X minus mu divided by sigma‚Äù

Confidence Interval: ‚ÄúMean plus or minus Z times standard error‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúCI = xÃÑ ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó SE‚Äù

Chi-square: ‚ÄúSum of (Observed minus Expected) squared over Expected‚Äù - Remember: ‚Äúœá¬≤ = Œ£(O-E)¬≤/E‚Äù

Demography Formulas - Mnemonics

Crude Birth Rate: ‚ÄúBirths per thousand population‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúCBR = (Births/Population) √ó 1000‚Äù

Growth Rate: ‚ÄúBirth minus death plus migration‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúGR = CBR - CDR + Net Migration‚Äù

Sex Ratio: ‚ÄúMales per thousand females‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúSR = (Males/Females) √ó 1000‚Äù

Dependency Ratio: ‚ÄúDependents per hundred working age‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúDR = ((0-14 + 65+)/(15-64)) √ó 100‚Äù

Research Methodology - Mnemonics

FINER Criteria: ‚ÄúFind Interesting Novel Ethical Relevant‚Äù - F-easible, I-nteresting, N-ovel, E-thical, R-elevant

PICO Framework: ‚ÄúPopulation Intervention Comparison Outcome‚Äù - P-opulation, I-ntervention, C-omparison, O-utcome

Sample Size Components: ‚ÄúAlpha Beta Sigma Delta‚Äù - ZŒ± (significance), ZŒ≤ (power), œÉ (standard deviation), Œ¥ (difference)

Bias Types: ‚ÄúSelection, Information, Confounding‚Äù - Remember: ‚ÄúSIC‚Äù for systematic errors

Summary Tables for Quick Revision

Statistical Tests Comparison

Test Purpose Data Type Groups Assumptions
t-test Compare 2 means Continuous Independent/Paired Normality
Chi-square Test association Categorical 2+ groups Expected ‚â•5
ANOVA Compare 3+ means Continuous Independent groups Normality, equal variance
Mann-Whitney Compare 2 groups Ordinal/Continuous Independent Non-parametric
Kruskal-Wallis Compare 3+ groups Ordinal/Continuous Independent Non-parametric
Fisher‚Äôs exact Association test Categorical 2x2 table Small samples
Pearson correlation Linear relationship Continuous Continuous Normality, linearity
Spearman correlation Monotonic relationship Ordinal/Continuous Continuous Monotonic

Appendix D: Statistical Formulas and Calculations

D.1 Descriptive Statistics

D.1.1 Measures of Central Tendency

Mean (Œº or xÃÑ):

Œº = Œ£x / n
xÃÑ = Œ£x / n

Where: - Œ£x = sum of all values - n = number of values

Median (M): - For odd number of observations: Middle value - For even number of observations: Average of two middle values

Mode: - Most frequently occurring value

D.1.2 Measures of Dispersion

Range:

R = Maximum value - Minimum value

Variance (œÉ¬≤ or s¬≤):

œÉ¬≤ = Œ£(x - Œº)¬≤ / n  (population)
s¬≤ = Œ£(x - xÃÑ)¬≤ / (n-1)  (sample)

Standard Deviation (œÉ or s):

œÉ = ‚àöœÉ¬≤  (population)
s = ‚àös¬≤  (sample)

Coefficient of Variation (CV):

CV = (Standard deviation / Mean) √ó 100

D.2 Inferential Statistics

D.2.1 Confidence Intervals

For Mean (Large Sample, n ‚â• 30):

CI = xÃÑ ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó (s/‚àön)

Where: - xÃÑ = sample mean - Z(Œ±/2) = 1.96 for 95% CI, 2.576 for 99% CI - s = sample standard deviation - n = sample size

For Proportion:

CI = p ¬± Z(Œ±/2) √ó ‚àö[p(1-p)/n]

Where p = sample proportion

For Small Samples (n < 30): Use t-distribution instead of Z:

CI = xÃÑ ¬± t(Œ±/2, df) √ó (s/‚àön)

D.2.2 Hypothesis Testing

Z-test for Mean (Large Sample):

Z = (xÃÑ - Œº‚ÇÄ) / (s/‚àön)

t-test for Mean (Small Sample):

t = (xÃÑ - Œº‚ÇÄ) / (s/‚àön)
df = n - 1

Chi-square Test:

œá¬≤ = Œ£[(O - E)¬≤ / E]

Where: - O = Observed frequency - E = Expected frequency - df = (rows - 1) √ó (columns - 1)

Fisher‚Äôs Exact Test (2√ó2 table):

P = (a+b)! (c+d)! (a+c)! (b+d)! / (a! b! c! d! N!)

D.3 Correlation and Regression

D.3.1 Pearson Correlation Coefficient

r = Œ£[(x - xÃÑ)(y - »≥)] / ‚àö[Œ£(x - xÃÑ)¬≤ √ó Œ£(y - »≥)¬≤]

Properties: - r ranges from -1 to +1 - |r| = 1: Perfect correlation - r = 0: No linear correlation

D.3.2 Linear Regression

Simple Linear Regression:

Y = a + bX

Where: - a = Y-intercept = »≥ - bxÃÑ - b = slope = r √ó (s_y/s_x)

Multiple Linear Regression:

Y = b‚ÇÄ + b‚ÇÅX‚ÇÅ + b‚ÇÇX‚ÇÇ + ... + b‚ÇôX‚Çô

D.4 Epidemiological Measures

D.4.1 Relative Risk (RR)

RR = [a/(a+b)] / [c/(c+d)]

D.4.2 Odds Ratio (OR)

OR = (a√ód) / (b√óc)

D.4.3 Attributable Risk (AR)

AR = Incidence in exposed - Incidence in unexposed

D.4.4 Population Attributable Risk (PAR)

PAR = Pe √ó (RR - 1) / [Pe √ó (RR - 1) + 1]

Where Pe = proportion exposed in population

D.4.5 Number Needed to Harm (NNH)

NNH = 1 / Attributable risk

D.4.6 Sensitivity and Specificity

Sensitivity = TP / (TP + FN)

Specificity = TN / (TN + FP)

Positive Predictive Value = TP / (TP + FP)

Negative Predictive Value = TN / (TN + FN)

Positive Likelihood Ratio = Sensitivity / (1 - Specificity)

Negative Likelihood Ratio = (1 - Sensitivity) / Specificity

Appendix E: Drug Dosages and Treatment Protocols

E.1 Common Antibiotic Dosages

E.1.1 Respiratory Infections

Amoxicillin: - Adults: 250-500 mg TDS for 5-7 days - Children: 40-90 mg/kg/day divided TID - Maximum: 4 g/day

Azithromycin: - Adults: 500 mg OD on day 1, then 250 mg OD on days 2-5 - Children: 10 mg/kg on day 1, then 5 mg/kg on days 2-5

Doxycycline: - Adults: 100 mg BD for 7-14 days - Contraindicated in children <8 years and pregnancy

E.1.2 Gastrointestinal Infections

Metronidazole: - Adults: 400 mg TDS for 5-7 days - Children: 30-50 mg/kg/day divided TID - Maximum: 2 g/day

Ciprofloxacin: - Adults: 500 mg BD for 3-5 days - Not recommended in children and pregnancy

E.2 Antimalarial Treatment

E.2.1 Uncomplicated Malaria

Plasmodium falciparum (ACT): - Artemether-lumefantrine: 4 tablets (each containing 20 mg artemether and 120 mg lumefantrine) at 0, 8, 24, and 36 hours, then twice daily for 2 more days

Plasmodium vivax: - Chloroquine: 25 mg/kg divided over 3 days + Primaquine: 0.25 mg/kg daily for 14 days (for radical cure)

E.2.2 Severe Malaria

Artesunate: - IV: 2.4 mg/kg at 0, 12, 24 hours, then daily - Alternative: Quinine: 20 mg/kg loading dose IV over 4 hours

E.3 Tuberculosis Treatment (WHO Guidelines)

E.3.1 New Cases (Category I)

Intensive Phase (2 months): - Isoniazid (H): 300 mg OD - Rifampicin (R): 450 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 600 mg OD (‚â•50 kg) - Ethambutol (E): 800 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 1200 mg OD (‚â•50 kg) - Pyrazinamide (Z): 1000 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 1500 mg OD (‚â•50 kg)

Continuation Phase (4 months): - Isoniazid (H): 300 mg OD - Rifampicin (R): 450 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 600 mg OD (‚â•50 kg)

E.3.2 Retreatment Cases (Category II)

Intensive Phase (3 months): - Streptomycin (S): 750 mg IM OD - Isoniazid (H): 300 mg OD - Rifampicin (R): 450 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 600 mg OD (‚â•50 kg) - Ethambutol (E): 800 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 1200 mg OD (‚â•50 kg) - Pyrazinamide (Z): 1000 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 1500 mg OD (‚â•50 kg)

Continuation Phase (5 months): - Isoniazid (H): 300 mg OD - Rifampicin (R): 450 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 600 mg OD (‚â•50 kg) - Ethambutol (E): 800 mg OD (weight <50 kg), 1200 mg OD (‚â•50 kg)

E.4 HIV/AIDS Treatment (First Line)

E.4.1 First-line ART Regimen

Adults and Adolescents: - Tenofovir (TDF) + Lamivudine (3TC) + Efavirenz (EFV) - TDF: 300 mg OD - 3TC: 300 mg OD - EFV: 600 mg OD

Children (3-10 years): - ABC + 3TC + EFV - Weight-based dosing per WHO guidelines

E.4.2 Opportunistic Infection Prophylaxis

Cotrimoxazole Prophylaxis: - CD4 <350 cells/ŒºL: 960 mg daily - Prevention of PCP and bacterial infections

E.5 Chronic Disease Management

E.5.1 Hypertension (JNC 8 Guidelines)

Thiazide Diuretics: - Hydrochlorothiazide: 12.5-50 mg daily - Chlorthalidone: 12.5-25 mg daily

ACE Inhibitors: - Enalapril: 5-40 mg daily in divided doses - Lisinopril: 10-40 mg daily

Calcium Channel Blockers: - Amlodipine: 2.5-10 mg daily - Nifedipine XL: 30-90 mg daily

E.5.2 Diabetes Mellitus (ADA Guidelines)

Metformin: - Initial: 500 mg BD with meals - Maximum: 2000 mg/day

Insulin Therapy: - Basal insulin: Long-acting (glargine, detemir) - Mealtime insulin: Rapid-acting (aspart, lispro)

E.6 Emergency Medications

E.6.1 Anaphylaxis Treatment

Epinephrine: - IM: 0.3-0.5 mg (1:1000) in anterolateral thigh - Repeat every 5-15 minutes if needed - IV: 0.05-0.1 mg (1:10000) for severe cases

Hydrocortisone: - IV: 200 mg slowly - Followed by oral prednisolone: 40-60 mg daily

E.6.2 Acute Asthma Management

Short-acting Beta-2 Agonists: - Salbutamol: 2.5-5 mg via nebulizer - Maximum: Every 20 minutes for 1 hour

Systemic Corticosteroids: - Prednisolone: 1-2 mg/kg (max 40-60 mg) - Duration: 5-10 days

Appendix H: Glossary of Medical Terms

H.1 Epidemiological Terms

Attack Rate The proportion of susceptible individuals who become ill during a specified period. Calculated as: (Number of new cases during period / Total susceptible population at risk) √ó 100.

Attributable Risk The difference in incidence rates between exposed and unexposed groups. Represents the excess risk that can be attributed to the exposure.

Case Fatality Rate (CFR) The proportion of diagnosed cases that result in death. Calculated as: (Number of deaths from disease / Number of diagnosed cases) √ó 100.

Endemic A disease or condition that is constantly present in a particular geographic area or population group at a baseline level.

Epidemic The occurrence of cases of a disease in excess of what is normally expected in a given population in a specific area.

Incidence The number of new cases of a disease that develop in a population during a specific period. Usually expressed as cases per 1,000 or 100,000 population per year.

Mortality Rate The frequency of death in a defined population during a specified time period. Expressed as deaths per 1,000 or 100,000 population per year.

Outbreak A localized epidemic, often referring to cases clustered in time and space in a specific population.

Pandemic An epidemic occurring worldwide or over a very wide area, crossing international boundaries and affecting a large number of people.

Prevalence The total number of cases of a disease existing in a population at a specific time point or during a specific period.

Risk Factor An exposure or characteristic that increases the probability of developing a disease or condition.

H.2 Statistical Terms

Bias Systematic error in study design or analysis that results in an incorrect estimate of the true effect or association.

Confidence Interval (CI) A range of values that is likely to contain the true population parameter with a specified level of confidence (usually 95%).

Confounding A situation where the apparent association between exposure and outcome is distorted by a third variable associated with both.

Correlation A statistical measure that describes the strength and direction of a relationship between two variables.

Frequency Distribution The pattern of how often different values occur in a dataset.

Hypothesis Testing A statistical procedure used to determine whether there is sufficient evidence to reject a null hypothesis.

Null Hypothesis (H‚ÇÄ) The hypothesis that there is no effect or no association between variables being studied.

P-value The probability of obtaining results at least as extreme as those observed, assuming the null hypothesis is true.

Probability The likelihood that an event will occur, expressed as a number between 0 and 1.

Sample Size The number of subjects or observations included in a study.

Statistical Significance The likelihood that a relationship between variables is not due to chance, typically determined when p < 0.05.

Type I Error Rejecting the null hypothesis when it is actually true (false positive).

Type II Error Failing to reject the null hypothesis when it is actually false (false negative).

H.3 Public Health Terms

Burden of Disease The impact of a health problem on a population, measured by mortality, morbidity, disability, and economic costs.

Community Health The health status of a defined group of people and the health actions they take to improve or maintain health.

Demography The study of populations, including size, composition, distribution, and trends.

Determinants of Health Factors that influence health status, including biological, environmental, social, and economic factors.

Epidemiology The study of distribution and determinants of health-related states in populations and the application to control health problems.

Health Promotion The process of enabling people to increase control over and improve their health.

Incidence Rate The rate at which new cases occur in a population during a specific time period.

Morbidity The state of being diseased or the amount of disease in a population.

Mortality The frequency of death in a population during a specific time period.

Population Health The health outcomes of a group of individuals, including the distribution of such outcomes within the group.

Primary Prevention Prevention strategies aimed at avoiding the development of disease before it occurs.

Secondary Prevention Early detection and treatment of disease to prevent progression and complications.

Tertiary Prevention Measures to reduce the impact of established disease and prevent further complications.

Surveillance The ongoing, systematic collection, analysis, and interpretation of health data for planning, implementation, and evaluation of public health practice.

H.4 Clinical Terms

Acute Having a rapid onset and usually a short course; often severe but brief.

Asymptomatic Showing no signs or symptoms of disease.

Carrier A person or animal that harbors a specific infectious agent and can transmit it to others without showing signs of disease.

Chronic Long-lasting or recurrent, often persisting for months or years.

Comorbidity The presence of one or more additional diseases or conditions co-occurring with a primary disease or condition.

Differential Diagnosis The process by which a physician determines which of two or more diseases or conditions a patient has, based on specific signs and symptoms.

Follow-up The continued observation and care of a patient after initial treatment.

Incubation Period The time between exposure to a pathogen and the onset of symptoms.

Latent Period The time between infection and the ability to transmit the infection to others.

Morbidity Rate The frequency of disease in a population, expressed as cases per 1,000 or 100,000 population.

Pathogen A microorganism capable of causing disease.

Prognosis The likely course and outcome of a disease or condition.

Risk Assessment The evaluation of the likelihood that exposure to a hazard will result in harm.

Spectrum of Disease The range of manifestations of disease, from asymptomatic infection to severe disease.

Survivorship The condition of living with or recovering from a serious disease or condition.

Vector An organism, typically an arthropod, that transmits a pathogen from one host to another.

Virulence The severity or harmfulness of a disease or condition.

H.5 Environmental Health Terms

Air Quality Index (AQI) A scale used to communicate how polluted the air currently is or how polluted it is forecast to become.

Bioaccumulation The accumulation of substances, such as pesticides or chemicals, in an organism over time.

Contamination The action of making something impure by exposure to a harmful substance.

Environmental Exposure Contact with potentially harmful substances in the environment.

Environmental Health The science and practice of preventing human injury and illness and promoting well-being by identifying and evaluating environmental sources and hazardous agents.

Exposure Assessment The process of estimating or measuring the intensity, frequency, and duration of human contact with an agent.

Hazard A biological, chemical, or physical agent that is reasonably likely to cause harm under some circumstances.

Pollution The presence in the environment of a substance or condition that causes harm.

Risk Assessment The process of evaluating the potential risks associated with a hazard.

Sanitation The provision of facilities and services for the safe disposal of human waste and the maintenance of hygienic conditions.

Waste Management The collection, transport, processing, recycling, or disposal of waste materials.

Water Quality The physical, chemical, biological, and radiological characteristics of water.
